## Transcript

- [00:00] So, the name Ivan Pavlov rings a bell it's because his experiments are among
- [00:05] the most famous in the history of psychology. His work contributed to the
- [00:09] foundation of the behaviorist school of thought that viewed psychology as an
- [00:13] empirically rigorous science, focused on observable behaviors and not
- [00:17] unobservable internal mental processes. Even though today we view psychology as
- [00:21] the science of both behavior and mental processes, Pavlov's influence was
- [00:25] tremendous. His research helped pave the path for
- [00:28] more experimental rigor and behavioral research right up to the present day.
- [00:32] Born in 1849 in Russia, Pavlov was never much for psychology. After giving up on
- [00:37] his original aspiration to become a Russian Orthodox priest like his father,
- [00:41] he instead earned a medical degree and spent nearly 20 years studying the
- [00:46] digestive system, earning Russia's first Nobel Prize in his mid-50s for his
- [00:50] research expanding our understanding of how stomachs worked. He didn't study
- [00:54] human stomachs, though, because the procedures were terrible and cruel.
- [00:59] He studied dog stomachs. And while researching those dogs, he noticed how
- [01:03] the animals would salivate at a mere whiff of their dinner. At first, he found
- [01:07] all that slobber annoying, but soon started to suspect that this behavior
- [01:11] was actually a simple but important form of learning. For us scholars of
- [01:15] psychology, we can define learning as the process of acquiring, through experience,
- [01:19] new and relatively enduring information or behaviors. Whether through association,
- [01:24] observation, or just plain thinking, learning is what allows us to adapt to
- [01:29] our environments and to survive. And as Pavlov began to discover, it wasn't only
- [01:33] humans who learned. Soon enough, he was turning out his famous series of
- [01:37] experiments in which he paired the presence of meat powder -- yummy -- which got
- [01:41] the dogs to drooling, with lots of different neutral stimuli. Things that
- [01:45] wouldn't normally make you drool, like a certain sound or shining a light or a
- [01:49] touch on the leg. Then Pavlov observed, after several of these pairings, a dog
- [01:53] would start to drool just at the sound or the light or the touch, even if there
- [01:57] wasn't any slobber-inducing meat powder around. Animals, he found, can exhibit
- [02:01] associative learning. That's when a subject links certain events, behaviors,
- [02:05] or stimuli together in the process of conditioning. This may be the most
- [02:09] elemental, basic form of learning a brain can do, but that doesn't mean that the
- [02:12] processes behind conditioning are, or ever were, obvious,
- [02:16] or, for that matter, simple.
- [02:17] In fact, the research that's gone into how we're conditioned by our environments has helped shape
- [02:21] the science of psychology from a still-kinda-subjective thought
- [02:25] exercise into the more rigorous discipline we know today.
- [02:28] And it starred some of psychology's most notable, and often controversial, figures
- [02:32] including Pavlov, BF Skinner, and that guy who trained kids to be terrified of furry animals.
- [02:38] [INTRO MUSIC]
- [02:47] Okay, I'm not a licensed dog trainer (do they license dog trainers?),
- [02:51] but I can break down for you the sequence of steps in Pavlov's famous experiment to help
- [02:55] you get a sense of how conditioning works. First, before conditioning, the dog
- [02:59] just drools when it smells food. That smell is the unconditioned stimulus and
- [03:03] the slobbering, the unconditioned or natural response. The ringing sound, which
- [03:08] at this point means nothing to the dog, is the neutral stimulus, and it produces
- [03:12] no drooling. During conditioning, the unconditioned stimulus -- the food smell --
- [03:16] is paired with the neutral stimulus -- the bell sound -- and results in drooling.
- [03:20] This is repeated many times until the association between the two stimuli is
- [03:24] made in a stage called acquisition. By the time you get to the after-conditioning
- [03:28] phase, that old neutral stimulus has become a conditioned stimulus, because it now elicits
- [03:34] the conditioned response of drooling. Sounds super simple, right?
- [03:37] If you have a dog, you've probably seen it tap-dance at the sight of a leash, but in
- [03:41] Pavlov's day, this whole series of steps hadn't really been studied in a lab setting, or even
- [03:46] thought about in scientific terms. Pavlov's work suggested that classical conditioning,
- [03:49] as this kind of learning came to be known, could be an adaptive form of learning that
- [03:54] helps an animal survive by changing its behavior to better suit its environment. In this case,
- [03:59] a bell means food, and food means survival, so get ready! Not only, that but
- [04:03] methodologically, classical conditioning shows how a process like learning can
- [04:07] actually be studied through direct observation of behavior in real-time,
- [04:11] without all those messy feelings and emotions. This is something Pavlov
- [04:15] especially appreciated, given his disdain for "mentalistic concepts" like
- [04:19] consciousness and introspection, championed by Freud. Behaviorist
- [04:23] psychologists like Pavlov's younger American analogs, BF Skinner and John B
- [04:27] Watson, also embraced the notion that psychology was all about objective,
- [04:31] observable behavior. In his 1930 book, Behaviorism, he argued that given a dozen
- [04:35] healthy infants, he could train any one of them to be a doctor, artist, lawyer, or
- [04:39] even a thief, regardless of their talents, tendencies, or ancestry.
- [04:43] Woah there, Watson! Thankfully, no one gave him any infants.
- [04:46] In his most famous, and yes, controversial experiment,
- [04:49] Watson conditioned a young child dubbed
- [04:51] "Little Albert" to fear a white rat. Maybe that doesn't sound so bad, but he
- [04:56] accomplished this by pairing the rat with a loud scary noise over and over.
- [05:00] And then demonstrated that the terror could branch out and be generalized to include
- [05:04] other furry white objects, like bunnies, dogs, and even fur coats. So yeah, that
- [05:09] would not fly today, obviously. But Watson's research did make other psychologists wonder
- [05:14] whether adults, too, were just holding tanks of conditioned emotions. And if so, whether new
- [05:20] conditioning could be used to undo old conditioning. Like, if you're terrified of
- [05:24] rollercoasters, but you made yourself ride one ten times a day for two weeks,
- [05:27] would your fears fade? For the record, recent exploration has revealed that the boy
- [05:31] known as "Little Albert" sadly died a few years after these experiments, while
- [05:36] Watson eventually left academia and got into advertising, where he put all that
- [05:40] associative learning to lucrative use. So that's classical conditioning, but we've
- [05:44] also got another kind of associative learning: operant conditioning.
- [05:48] If classical conditioning is all about forming associations between stimuli,
- [05:51] operant conditioning involves associating our own behavior with
- [05:55] consequences. The kid who gets a cookie for saying "please," or the aquarium seal
- [05:59] that gets a sardine for balancing a ball on its nose -- they've both been trained
- [06:03] with operant conditioning. The basic premise here is that behaviors increase
- [06:07] when followed by reinforcement or reward, but they decrease when followed by
- [06:11] punishment. And the most well-known champion of operant conditioning is
- [06:14] American behaviorist, B.F. Skinner. He designed the famous operant chamber or
- [06:19] "Skinner Box" -- a confined space containing a lever or button than an animal can touch
- [06:24] to get some kind of reward (typically food), along with the device that keeps track of its
- [06:28] responses. Okay, time for a debunking break. Other than maybe Freud, no other figure in
- [06:33] psychology seems to be as shrouded in lore and misinformation as B.F. Skinner.
- [06:38] So I'm just gonna tell you straight that no, Skinner never put any kids in the box,
- [06:43] and no, he didn't raise his children without love or affection. And his daughter didn't
- [06:48] hate his guts until the day she committed suicide. Deborah Skinner is alive and well and she loved
- [06:54] her dad plenty. Skinner did, however, invent
- [06:56] something called an "Air Crib" -- a climate-controlled
- [06:58] box with a window on the front that was meant to keep babies warm and safe while their moms ran
- [07:04] around doing their 1950s lady thang. It's not exactly where I'd like to spend the night, but it
- [07:08] wasn't remotely the same as the Skinner Box. No one knows where all these myths came
- [07:13] from, but being a somewhat controversial guy, Skinner had a lot of haters. Some of whom
- [07:17] were probably happy to perpetuate misinformation. But back to the rat in the box. Basically, the box
- [07:21] provided an observable stage to demonstrate Skinner's concept of reinforcement, which is
- [07:26] anything that increases the behavior that it follows.
- [07:29] In other words, you push the lever, you get a snack,
- [07:31] and then you want to keep pushing the lever. But most rats aren't gonna push a lever for
- [07:36] no reason. I mean, there aren't food dispensing levers in the natural environment. So operant
- [07:41] conditioning behavior requires shaping. Maybe you give the rat a little nibble of food each time
- [07:45] it gets closer to the bar, then only when it touches
- [07:47] the bar, until little by little, in a series of
- [07:49] successive approximations to the desired behavior, you reward them only when they
- [07:53] do the thing you're trying to shape them to do. In everyday life, we're all continually
- [07:57] reinforcing, shaping, and refining each other's behaviors, both intentionally and accidentally.
- [08:02] We do this with both positive and negative reinforcement. Positive reinforcement
- [08:07] obviously strengthens responses by giving rewards after a desired event, like the rat snack
- [08:12] after the lever push, or getting a cookie when you say please. Negative reinforcement is a little
- [08:17] trickier -- it's what increases a behavior by taking
- [08:20] away an aversive or upsetting stimulus, like, say,
- [08:23] you get in your car and it does that infernal beeping thing until you fasten your seat belt.
- [08:27] The car is reinforcing your seatbelt-wearing by getting rid of that horrible beeping. And it's
- [08:32] good. You should wear your seatbelt. It's important to recognize here that negative
- [08:36] reinforcement is not the same as punishment.
- [08:38] Punishment decreases a behavior, either positively,
- [08:41] by, say, getting a speeding ticket, or negatively, by taking away a driver's license. But negative
- [08:46] reinforcement removes the punishing event to increase the behavior. So pain killers negatively
- [08:52] reinforce the behavior of swallowing them by ending the headache. So by now, hopefully, you're
- [08:56] getting the picture. There are things that we want and things that we don't want, and we can be
- [09:00] taught by way of those impulses to behave certain ways. But it's worth pointing out that
- [09:04] the conditioning is way more complex than just the cookie in the beeping car. For one thing,
- [09:09] ending annoyance or getting a cookie are types
- [09:12] of primary reinforcers. You don't have to learn that;
- [09:15] they just make innate biological sense. Beeping is
- [09:18] annoying; cookies are delicious. So there are other
- [09:20] kinds of reinforcers that we only recognize after
- [09:22] we learn to associate them with primary reinforcers,
- [09:25] like a paycheck is a conditioned reinforcer. We want money because we need food and
- [09:30] shelter, which are still the primary drivers, plus just as there are different kinds of reinforcers,
- [09:35] so are there various reinforcement schedules.
- [09:38] Like, those box rats were getting continuous reinforcement when they got a treat every
- [09:42] single time they hit the lever, so they picked it up
- [09:45] pretty quickly. But if one day, the rat chow
- [09:46] doesn't come, that connection quickly dwindles and the rat stops hitting the lever. This is a
- [09:51] process called extinction, and it is important because that's how real life works. Outside
- [09:57] of a Skinner Box, you're not gonna get continuous reinforcement. All of life is a
- [10:01] series of partial or intermittent reinforcements that only occurs sometimes. Learning under these
- [10:06] conditions takes longer, but it holds up better in the long run, and is less
- [10:11] susceptible to that extinction. So, say a cafe gives out a free cup of coffee for
- [10:14] every 10 you buy, while another shop pours a free double shot every Tuesday
- [10:18] morning, and yet another has a free coffee lottery that customers win at
- [10:22] random. These are all different kinds of intermittent reinforcement techniques
- [10:26] that get customers coming back for more. Now, Pavlov, Watson, and Skinner's ideas
- [10:30] were definitely controversial, as well as the whole scary rat experiment, plenty of
- [10:35] folks disagreed with their insistence that only external influences and not
- [10:38] internal thoughts and feelings shaped behavior. It was clear to many of the
- [10:42] behaviorists' rivals that our cognitive processes -- our thoughts, perceptions,
- [10:46] feelings, memories -- also influence the way we learn. We're gonna talk about how
- [10:50] these other things factor into learning next week, when we look more at
